<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:International_Diplomacy_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/International_Diplomacy_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-International_Diplomacy_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/International_Diplomacy_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-International_Diplomacy_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/International_Diplomacy_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-International_Diplomacy_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="605" data-file-height="442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:International_Diplomacy_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Macmillan and Eisenhower meet in March 1957 for talks in <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, aiming to repair Anglo-American relationships in the aftermath of the previous year's <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Once he took office, Macmillan worked to undo the strain that the Special Relationship had incurred in the preceding years.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_48-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Macmillan famously quipped that it was Britain's historical duty to guide the power of the US as the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> had the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> He endeavoured to broaden the Special Relationship beyond Churchill's conception of an English-Speaking Union into a more inclusive "Atlantic Community".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> His key theme, "of the <a href="/wiki/Interdependence" class="mw-redirect" title="Interdependence">interdependence</a> of the nations of the <a href="/wiki/Free_World" title="Free World">Free World</a> and the partnership which must be maintained between Europe and the United States", was one that Kennedy subsequently took up.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Eisenhower increased tension with the UK by <a href="/wiki/1960_Paris_Summit" class="mw-redirect" title="1960 Paris Summit">sabotaging</a> Macmillan's policy of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="Détente">détente</a> with the Soviet Union at the May 1960 Paris summit.<sup id="cite_ref-ashton1_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashton1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span id="Macmillan_and_Kennedy_.28January_1961.E2.80.93October_1963.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Macmillan_and_Kennedy_(January_1961–October_1963)">Macmillan and Kennedy (January 1961–October 1963)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Macmillan and Kennedy (January 1961–October 1963)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg/200px-MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg/300px-MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg/400px-MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5725" data-file-height="4501" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MacMillian_and_Kennedy_in_Key_West_March_1961.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Macmillan and Kennedy at Key West in 1961</div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy was an <a href="/wiki/Anglophile" title="Anglophile">anglophile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_48-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Kennedy_Sr." title="Joseph P. Kennedy Sr.">father</a> had previously served as the US ambassador to the UK and his <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Cavendish,_Marchioness_of_Hartington" title="Kathleen Cavendish, Marchioness of Hartington">sister</a> had been Marchioness of Hartington, whose husband was incidentally Macmillan's nephew by marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_48-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>British intelligence assisted the US in assessing the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_missile_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuban missile crisis">Cuban missile crisis</a>. Kennedy appreciated Macmillan's steady leadership, and admired his <a href="/wiki/Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty">Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_48-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Skybolt_crisis">Skybolt crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Skybolt crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Special Relationship was perhaps tested the most severely by the <a href="/wiki/GAM-87_Skybolt#Cancellation" title="GAM-87 Skybolt">Skybolt crisis</a> of 1962, when Kennedy cancelled a joint project without consultation. Skybolt was a nuclear air-to-ground missile that could penetrate Soviet airspace and would extend the life of Britain's deterrent, which consisted only of free-falling hydrogen bombs. London saw cancellation as a reduction in the British <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom#Thermonuclear_weaponry" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear deterrent</a>. The crisis was resolved during a series of compromises that led to the Royal Navy purchasing the American UGM-27 Polaris missile and construction of the Resolution-class submarines to launch them.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The debates over Skybolt were top secret, but tensions were exacerbated when <a href="/wiki/Dean_Acheson" title="Dean Acheson">Dean Acheson</a>, a former Secretary of State, challenged publicly the Special Relationship and marginalised the British contribution to the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">Western alliance</a>. Acheson said:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Great Britain has lost an empire and has not yet found a role. The attempt to play a separate power role—that is, a role apart from Europe, a role based on a 'Special Relationship' with the United States, a role based on being the head of a '<a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>' which has no political structure, or unity, or strength and enjoys a fragile and precarious economic relationship—this role is about played out.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg/170px-PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg/255px-PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg/340px-PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1236" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PolarisMissileIWMLondon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">UGM-27 Polaris</a> missile at the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_War_Museum" title="Imperial War Museum">Imperial War Museum</a> in London</div></div></div>
<p>On learning of Acheson's attack, Macmillan thundered in public:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In so far as he appeared to denigrate the resolution and will of Britain and the British people, Mr. Acheson has fallen into an error which has been made by quite a lot of people in the course of the last four hundred years, including <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip of Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV_of_France" title="Louis IV of France">Louis XIV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I">Napoleon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilhelm II of Germany">Kaiser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a>. He also seems to misunderstand the role of the Commonwealth in world affairs. In so far as he referred to Britain's attempt to play a separate power role as about to be played out, this would be acceptable if he had extended this concept to the US and to every other nation in the Free World. This is the doctrine of interdependence, which must be applied in the world today, if Peace and Prosperity are to be assured. I do not know whether Mr. Acheson would accept the logical sequence of his own argument. I am sure it is fully recognised by the US administration and by the American people.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The looming collapse of the alliance between the two thermonuclear powers forced Kennedy into an about-face at the Anglo-American summit in <a href="/wiki/Nassau,_Bahamas" title="Nassau, Bahamas">Nassau</a>, where he <a href="/wiki/Nassau_Agreement" title="Nassau Agreement">agreed</a> to sell <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris#British_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">Polaris</a> as a replacement for the cancelled Skybolt. <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Neustadt" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard E. Neustadt">Richard E. Neustadt</a> in his official investigation concluded the crisis in the Special Relationship had erupted because "the president's 'Chiefs' failed to make a proper strategic assessment of Great Britain's intentions and its capabilities".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Skybolt crisis with Kennedy came on top of Eisenhower's wrecking of Macmillan's policy of détente with the Soviet Union at the May 1960 Paris summit, and the prime minister's resulting disenchantment with the Special Relationship contributed to his decision to seek an alternative in British membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC).<sup id="cite_ref-ashton1_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashton1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> According to a recent analyst: "What the prime minister in effect adopted was a <i>hedging strategy</i> in which ties with Washington would be maintained while at the same time a new power base in Europe was sought."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Even so, Kennedy assured Macmillan "that relations between the United States and the UK would be strengthened not weakened, if the UK moved towards membership."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Douglas-Home_and_Kennedy_.28October.E2.80.93November_1963.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Douglas-Home_and_Kennedy_(October–November_1963)">Douglas-Home and Kennedy (October–November 1963)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Douglas-Home and Kennedy (October–November 1963)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-AR7514-C_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/JFKWHP-AR7514-C_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-JFKWHP-AR7514-C_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/JFKWHP-AR7514-C_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-JFKWHP-AR7514-C_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/JFKWHP-AR7514-C_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-JFKWHP-AR7514-C_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="461" data-file-height="528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-AR7514-C_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy hosts (then-Foreign Secretary) Douglas-Home at the White House in 1962</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-KN-C30678_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/JFKWHP-KN-C30678_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-JFKWHP-KN-C30678_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/JFKWHP-KN-C30678_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-JFKWHP-KN-C30678_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/JFKWHP-KN-C30678_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="367" data-file-height="393" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-KN-C30678_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Douglas-Home converses with Kennedy's widow <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis">Jacqueline</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted</a> at a reception in the White House <a href="/wiki/Cross_Hall" title="Cross Hall">Cross Hall</a> following <a href="/wiki/State_funeral_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="State funeral of John F. Kennedy">Kennedy's state funeral</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a> only entered the race to replace the resigning Macmillan as Leader of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> after learning from the British ambassador to the US that the Kennedy administration was uneasy at the prospect of <a href="/wiki/Quintin_Hogg,_Baron_Hailsham_of_St_Marylebone" title="Quintin Hogg, Baron Hailsham of St Marylebone">Hailsham</a> being Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Douglas-Home, however, would only serve as Prime Minister for a little over a month before Kennedy <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">was assassinated</a>.
</p><p>In England, Kennedy's assassination in November 1963 caused a profound shock and sadness expressed by many politicians, religious leaders, and luminaries of literature and the arts. The Archbishop of Canterbury led a memorial service at St Paul's Cathedral. Sir <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a> at the end of his next performance called for a moment of silence, followed by a playing of "The Star Spangled Banner." Prime Minister Douglas-Home led parliamentary tributes to Kennedy, whom he called, "the most loyal and faithful of allies."<sup id="cite_ref-cookclive_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cookclive-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Douglas-Home was visibly upset during his remarks, as he was truly saddened by Kennedy's death. He had liked Kennedy, and had begun to establish a positive working relationship with him.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After his assassination, the British government sought approval to build a memorial to President Kennedy, in part to demonstrate the strength of the Special Relationship. However, the weak popular response to its ambitious fundraising campaign was a surprise, and suggested a grassroots opposition to the late president, his policies and the US.<sup id="cite_ref-cookclive_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cookclive-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Douglas-Home_and_Johnson_.28November_1963.E2.80.93October_1964.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Douglas-Home_and_Johnson_(November_1963–October_1964)">Douglas-Home and Johnson (November 1963–October 1964)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Douglas-Home and Johnson (November 1963–October 1964)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-KN-C18825_(cropped1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/JFKWHP-KN-C18825_%28cropped1%29.jpg/220px-JFKWHP-KN-C18825_%28cropped1%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/JFKWHP-KN-C18825_%28cropped1%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="284" data-file-height="344" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFKWHP-KN-C18825_(cropped1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1961 photograph of then-Vice President Johnson and then-Foreign Secretary Douglas-Home in the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Room_(White_House)" title="Blue Room (White House)">Blue Room (White House)</a></div></div></div>
<p>Douglas-Home had a far more terse relationship with Kennedy's successor, <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>. Douglas-Home failed to develop a good relationship with Lyndon Johnson. Their governments had a serious disagreement on the question of British trade with Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Relations between the two nations worsened after <a href="/wiki/British_Leyland" title="British Leyland">British Leyland</a> busses were sold to <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-douglasgov_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-douglasgov-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> thus undermining the effectiveness of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_embargo_against_Cuba" title="United States embargo against Cuba">United States embargo against Cuba</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-douglasgov_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-douglasgov-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Douglas' Conservative Party lost the <a href="/wiki/1964_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1964 United Kingdom general election">1964 general election</a>, thus he lost his position as Prime Minister. He had only served as Prime Minister for 363 days, the U.K.'s second shortest premiership of the twentieth century. Despite its unusual brevity (and due to the assassination of Kennedy), Douglas-Home's tenure had overlapped with two US presidencies.<sup id="cite_ref-douglasgov_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-douglasgov-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span id="Wilson_and_Johnson_.28October_1964.E2.80.93January_1969.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wilson_and_Johnson_(October_1964–January_1969)">Wilson and Johnson (October 1964–January 1969)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Wilson and Johnson (October 1964–January 1969)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4578" data-file-height="3463" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_meets_with_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_C2537-5_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wilson and Johnson meet at the White House in 1966</div></div></div>
<p>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> recast the alliance as a "close relationship",<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> but neither he nor President Lyndon B. Johnson had any direct experience of foreign policy,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> and Wilson's attempt to mediate in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam</a>, where the United Kingdom was co-chairman with the Soviet Union of the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conference_(1954)" class="mw-redirect" title="Geneva Conference (1954)">Geneva Conference</a>, was unwelcome to the president. "I won't tell you how to run <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and you don't tell us how to run Vietnam," Johnson snapped in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds,_p._14_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds,_p._14-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> However, relations were sustained by U.S. recognition that Wilson was being criticised at home by his neutralist <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> left for not condemning American involvement in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Hara,_Review,_p._482_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Hara,_Review,_p._482-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>U.S. Defense Secretary <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNamara" title="Robert McNamara">Robert McNamara</a> asked Britain to send troops to Vietnam as 'the unwritten terms of the Special Relationship',<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Wilson agreed to help in many ways but refused to commit regular forces, only <a href="/wiki/Special_forces" title="Special forces">special forces</a> instructors. <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> did commit regular forces to Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Johnson administration's support for <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a> loans delayed devaluation of <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">sterling</a> until 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Hara,_Review,_p._482_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Hara,_Review,_p._482-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The United Kingdom's subsequent withdrawal from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and East Asia surprised Washington, where it was strongly opposed because British forces were valued for their contribution.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In retrospect Wilson's moves to scale back Britain's global commitments and correct its balance of payments contrasted with Johnson's overexertions which accelerated the relative economic and military decline of the US.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Hara,_Review,_p._482_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Hara,_Review,_p._482-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Wilson_and_Nixon_.28January_1969.E2.80.93June_1970.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wilson_and_Nixon_(January_1969–June_1970)">Wilson and Nixon (January 1969–June 1970)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Wilson and Nixon (January 1969–June 1970)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif/lossy-page1-220px-White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif/lossy-page1-330px-White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif/lossy-page1-440px-White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_House_Guards_and_Military_Band_members_arrayed_for_the_visit_of_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_of_Great_Britain_-_NARA_-_194306.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wilson visiting the White House in January 1970</div></div></div>
<p>By the time <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> had taken office, many issues of tension between the two nations had been resolved. This allowed for the Special Relationship to blossom.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a speech delivered on 27 January 1970 at a state dinner welcoming the Prime Minister in his visit to the US Nixon said,
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Mr. Prime Minister, I am delighted to welcome you here today as an old friend; as an old friend not only in government, but as an old friend personally. I noted from reading the background, that this is your 21st visit to the United States, and your seventh visit as Prime Minister of your government.
</p><p>And I noted, too, in looking at the relationship that we have had since I assumed office a year ago, that we met twice in London, once in February, again in August; that we have had a great deal of correspondence; we have talked several times on the telephone. But what is even more important is the substance of those conversations. The substance did not involve differences between your country and ours. The substance of those conversations was with regard to the great issues in which we have a common interest and a common purpose, the development of peace in the world, progress for your people, for our people, for all people. This is the way it should be. This is the way we both want it. And it is an indication of the way to the future.
</p><p>Winston Churchill once said on one of his visits to this country that, if we are together, nothing is impossible. Perhaps in saying that nothing is impossible, that was an exaggeration. But it can be said today--we are together, and being together, a great deal is possible. And I am sure that our talks will make some of those things possible.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span id="Heath_and_Nixon_.28June_1970.E2.80.93March_1974.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Heath_and_Nixon_(June_1970–March_1974)">Heath and Nixon (June 1970–March 1974)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Heath and Nixon (June 1970–March 1974)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg/220px-Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg/330px-Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg/440px-Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="498" data-file-height="348" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_and_Pat_Nixon_with_Queen_Elizabeth_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> and Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> with President Richard M. Nixon and First Lady <a href="/wiki/Pat_Nixon" title="Pat Nixon">Pat Nixon</a> during the Nixons' 1970 visit to the United Kingdom</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Europeanism" title="Europeanism">Europeanist</a>, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> preferred to speak of a "'natural relationship', based on shared culture and heritage", and stressed that the Special Relationship was "not part of his own vocabulary".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Heath-<a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Nixon</a> era was dominated by the United Kingdom's 1973 entry into the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC). Although the two leaders' 1971 <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> communiqué restated that entry served the interests of the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">Atlantic Alliance</a>, American observers voiced concern that the British government's membership would impair its role as an honest broker, and that, because of the European goal of political union, the Special Relationship would only survive if it included the whole Community.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics accused President Nixon of impeding the EEC's inclusion in the Special Relationship by his economic policy,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> which dismantled the <a href="/wiki/Postwar" class="mw-redirect" title="Postwar">postwar</a> <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">international monetary system</a> and sought to force open European markets for US exports.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Detractors also slated the personal relationship at the top as "decidedly less than special"; Prime Minister Edward Heath, it was alleged, "hardly dared put through a phone call to Richard Nixon for fear of offending his new Common Market partners."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Special Relationship was "soured" during the <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Arab–Israeli War</a> of 1973 when Nixon failed to inform Heath that US forces had been put on <a href="/wiki/DEFCON" title="DEFCON">DEFCON</a> 3 in a worldwide standoff with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, and US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> misled the <a href="/wiki/Rowland_Baring,_3rd_Earl_of_Cromer" title="Rowland Baring, 3rd Earl of Cromer">British ambassador</a> over the nuclear alert.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Heath, who learned about the alert only from press reports hours later, confessed: "I have found considerable alarm as to what use the Americans would have been able to make of their forces here without in any way consulting us or considering the British interests."<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The incident marked "a low ebb" in the Special Relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Wilson_and_Nixon_.28March_1974.E2.80.93August_1974.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wilson_and_Nixon_(March_1974–August_1974)">Wilson and Nixon (March 1974–August 1974)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Wilson and Nixon (March 1974–August 1974)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels,_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels%2C_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif/lossy-page1-150px-President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels%2C_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels%2C_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif/lossy-page1-225px-President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels%2C_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels%2C_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif/lossy-page1-300px-President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels%2C_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2049" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Nixon_meets_with_British_Prime_Minister_Harold_P._Wilson_at_the_U.S._embassy_residence_in_Brussels,_Belgium_-_NARA_-_194589.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> (left) and President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> (right) in June 1974</div></div></div>
<p>Wilson and Nixon once again concurrently served as leaders of the two nations for a six-month period spanning from the start of Wilson's <a href="/wiki/Labour_government_1974%E2%80%9379" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour government 1974–79">second tenure as Prime Minister</a> until <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon%27s_resignation_speech" title="Richard Nixon&#39;s resignation speech">Nixon's resignation</a>. Wilson held Nixon in high regards. After he left office himself, Wilson praised Nixon as America's "most able" president.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Wilson_and_Ford_.28August_1974.E2.80.93April_1976.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wilson_and_Ford_(August_1974–April_1976)">Wilson and Ford (August 1974–April 1976)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Wilson and Ford (August 1974–April 1976)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_President_Gerald_R._Ford_and_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_of_Great_Britain_at_the_Arrival_Ceremony_for_the_Prime_Minister%27s_State_Visit_-_NARA_-_7518538.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Photograph_of_President_Gerald_R._Ford_and_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_of_Great_Britain_at_the_Arrival_Ceremony_for_the_Prime_Minister%27s_State_Visit_-_NARA_-_7518538.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Photograph_of_President_Gerald_R._Ford_and_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_of_Great_Britain_at_the_Arrival_Ceremony_for_the_Prime_Minister%27s_State_Visit_-_NARA_-_7518538.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Photograph_of_President_Gerald_R._Ford_and_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_of_Great_Britain_at_the_Arrival_Ceremony_for_the_Prime_Minister%27s_State_Visit_-_NARA_-_7518538.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4513" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_President_Gerald_R._Ford_and_Prime_Minister_Harold_Wilson_of_Great_Britain_at_the_Arrival_Ceremony_for_the_Prime_Minister%27s_State_Visit_-_NARA_-_7518538.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wilson and Ford in the <a href="/wiki/White_House_Rose_Garden" title="White House Rose Garden">White House Rose Garden</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> became president after Nixon's resignation. In a toast to Wilson at a January 1975 state dinner, Ford remarked,
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It gives me a very great deal of pleasure to welcome you again to the United States. You are no stranger, of course, to this city and to this house. Your visits here over the years as a staunch ally and a steadfast friend are continuing evidence of the excellence of the ties between our countries and our people.
</p><p>You, Mr. Prime Minister, are the honored leader of one of America's truest allies and oldest friends. Any student of American history and American culture knows how significant is our common heritage. We have actually continued to share a wonderful common history.
</p><p>Americans can never forget how the very roots of our democratic political system and of our concepts of liberty and government are to be found in Britain.
</p><p>Over the years, Britain and the United States have stood together as trusting friends and allies to defend the cause of freedom on a worldwide basis. Today, the North Atlantic Alliance remains the cornerstone of our common defense.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span id="Callaghan_and_Ford_.28April_1976.E2.80.93January_1977.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Callaghan_and_Ford_(April_1976–January_1977)">Callaghan and Ford (April 1976–January 1977)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Callaghan and Ford (April 1976–January 1977)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Callaghan_and_Ford_A0971_NLGRF_photo_contact_sheet_(1974-09-24)(Gerald_Ford_Library)_(01).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Callaghan_and_Ford_A0971_NLGRF_photo_contact_sheet_%281974-09-24%29%28Gerald_Ford_Library%29_%2801%29.jpg/150px-Callaghan_and_Ford_A0971_NLGRF_photo_contact_sheet_%281974-09-24%29%28Gerald_Ford_Library%29_%2801%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Callaghan_and_Ford_A0971_NLGRF_photo_contact_sheet_%281974-09-24%29%28Gerald_Ford_Library%29_%2801%29.jpg/225px-Callaghan_and_Ford_A0971_NLGRF_photo_contact_sheet_%281974-09-24%29%28Gerald_Ford_Library%29_%2801%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Callaghan_and_Ford_A0971_NLGRF_photo_contact_sheet_%281974-09-24%29%28Gerald_Ford_Library%29_%2801%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="173" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Callaghan_and_Ford_A0971_NLGRF_photo_contact_sheet_(1974-09-24)(Gerald_Ford_Library)_(01).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Callaghan and Ford sitting at the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a> fireplace</div></div></div>
<p>In April 1976, <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> became Prime Minister after Wilson <a href="/wiki/1976_Labour_Party_leadership_election" title="1976 Labour Party leadership election">resigned the office</a>. While President Ford never visited the United Kingdom during his presidency,<sup id="cite_ref-Glory&#39;,_1995_p._8_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glory&#39;,_1995_p._8-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> the British government saw the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">U.S. bicentennial</a> in 1976 as an occasion to celebrate the Special Relationship. Political leaders and guests from both sides of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> gathered in May at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Hall">Westminster Hall</a> to mark the American <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> of 1776. Prime Minister Callaghan presented a visiting Congressional delegation with a gold-embossed reproduction of <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, symbolising the common heritage of the two nations. British historian <a href="/wiki/Esmond_Wright" title="Esmond Wright">Esmond Wright</a> noted "a vast amount of popular identification with the American story". A year of cultural exchanges and exhibitions culminated in July in a state visit to the United States by the Queen.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford lost the <a href="/wiki/1976_United_States_presidential_election" title="1976 United States presidential election">1976 election</a>. Consequentially, his presidency ended in January 1977.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span id="Callaghan_and_Carter_.28January_1977.E2.80.93May_1979.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Callaghan_and_Carter_(January_1977–May_1979)">Callaghan and Carter (January 1977–May 1979)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Callaghan and Carter (January 1977–May 1979)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan,_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.,_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan%2C_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.%2C_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan%2C_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.%2C_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan%2C_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.%2C_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan%2C_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.%2C_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan%2C_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.%2C_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan%2C_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.%2C_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2934" data-file-height="2005" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_and_James_Callaghan,_Prime_Minister_of_Great_Britain.,_03-23-1978_-_NARA_-_178479.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> (left) and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> (right) in the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a> in March 1978</div></div></div>
<p>After defeating the incumbent Gerald Ford in the 1976 election, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> was sworn-in as President of the United States in January 1977. Ties between Callaghan and Carter were cordial but, with both left-of-centre governments being preoccupied with economic malaise, diplomatic contacts remained low key. US officials characterised relations in 1978 as "extremely good", with the main disagreement being over trans-Atlantic air routes.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The economic malaise that Callaghan was facing at home developed into the "<a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>", which ultimately led to Callaghan's Labour Party losing <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">the May 1979 general election</a>, thus ending his tenure as Prime Minister.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span id="Thatcher_and_Carter_.28May_1979.E2.80.93January_1981.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcher_and_Carter_(May_1979–January_1981)">Thatcher and Carter (May 1979–January 1981)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Thatcher and Carter (May 1979–January 1981)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_(cropped1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_%28cropped1%29.jpg/220px-Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_%28cropped1%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_%28cropped1%29.jpg/330px-Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_%28cropped1%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_%28cropped1%29.jpg/440px-Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_%28cropped1%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="859" data-file-height="515" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carters_with_Margaret_Thatcher_state_dinner_(cropped1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jimmy and <a href="/wiki/Rosalynn_Carter" title="Rosalynn Carter">Rosalynn Carter</a> hosting a state dinner for Margaret Thatcher at the White House during her 1979 visit to the United States</div></div></div>
<p>Conservative Party leader <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> became Prime Minister after her party won the general election in 1979. Relations between President Carter and Prime Minister Thatcher during the year-and-a-half overlap of their leadership have often been seen as relatively cold, especially when contrasted with the kinship that Thatcher would subsequently develop with Carter's successor <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gardianthatcher_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gardianthatcher-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> However, Carter's relationship with Thatcher never reached the levels of strain that Reagan's relationship would in the midst of the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-carterhistorynews_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carterhistorynews-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher and Carter had clear differences in their political ideology. They both occupied relatively opposing ends of the political spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> By the time she had become Prime Minister, Thatcher had already met Carter on two previous occasions. Both of these encounters had initially left Carter with a negative impression of her. However, his opinion of Thatcher had reportedly become more placid by the time she was elected Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the tensions between the two, historian Chris Collins (of the Margaret Thatcher Foundation) has stated, "Carter is somebody she worked hard to get along with. She had considerable success at it. Had Carter lasted two terms we might be writing about the surprising amount of common ground between the two."<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Carter congratulated Thatcher in a phone call after her party's victory in the <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">general election</a> (which elevated her to the office of Prime Minister), stating that the United States would, "look forward to working with you on an official basis." However, his congratulations was delivered with an audibly unenthusiastic tone.<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In her first full letter to Carter, Thatcher voiced her assurance of full support in the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks" title="Strategic Arms Limitation Talks">SALT II</a> nuclear arms treaty writing, "We will do all we can to assist you".<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after her election, following her first meeting with <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Israel" title="Prime Minister of Israel">Israeli Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Menachem_Begin" title="Menachem Begin">Menachem Begin</a> (which she would describe as, "profoundly disheartening"), Thatcher expressed her concerns to Carter about the issue of <a href="/wiki/Israeli_settlement" title="Israeli settlement">Israeli settlements</a> stating, "I emphasized to Mr. Begin the danger which continued expansion of Israeli settlements represents to the autonomy negotiations… but he will not listen and even resents the subject of settlements being raised at all."<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both leaders were mutually facing great pressures during the overlap of their tenures as a national leader. Both of their nations were experiencing economic crisis due to the <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession" title="Early 1980s recession">early 1980s recession</a>. In addition, there was international upheaval in Eastern Europe and the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Among the areas of turmoil were Afghanistan (due to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet–Afghan War">Soviet–Afghan War</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> and Iran (where Carter was facing a <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">hostage crisis</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1388" data-file-height="1512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Jimmy_Carter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carter with Thatcher having tea at the White House during her 1979 visit to the United States</div></div></div>
<p>Both Carter and Thatcher condemned the Soviet invasion of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> They expressed concern to each other that other European nations were being too soft towards the Russians. Carter hoped that she could persuade other European nations to condemn the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> However, with a particularly tumultuous economic situation at home, and with most NATO members reluctant to cut trade ties with the USSR, Thatcher would only provide very weak support to Carter's efforts to punish the USSR through economic sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was concerned that Carter was naive about Soviet relations.<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Thatcher played a (perhaps pivotal) role in fulfilling Carter's desire for the U.N. adoption of a resolution demanding the withdrawal of Soviet troops from Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-carterhistorynews_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carterhistorynews-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher also encouraged British athletes to participate in <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics_boycott" title="1980 Summer Olympics boycott">the boycott</a> of the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">1980 Summer Olympics</a> in Moscow, which Carter initiated in response to the invasion. However, Thatcher ultimately gave the <a href="/wiki/British_Olympic_Association" title="British Olympic Association">country's Olympic Committee</a> and individual athletes the choice to decide whether or not they would boycott the games. The United Kingdom ended up participating in the 1980 games, albeit with a smaller delegation due to individual athletes deciding to participate in boycotting the games.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-carterhistorynews_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carterhistorynews-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In their correspondences, Thatcher expressed sympathy to Carter's troubled efforts to resolve the hostage crisis in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> However, she outright refused his request for her to decrease the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_Kingdom,_Tehran" title="Embassy of the United Kingdom, Tehran">British embassy in Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher provided Carter with praise on his handling of the US economy, sending him a letter endorsing his measures in handling economic inflation and in cutting gas consumption during the <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="1979 energy crisis">1979 energy crisis</a> as, "painful but necessary".<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1979 Thatcher wrote Carter, "I share your concern about Cuban and Soviet intentions in the Caribbean. This danger exists more widely in the developing world. It is essential that the Soviet Union should recognise your resolve in this matter. […] I am therefore especially encouraged by your statement that you are accelerating efforts to increase the capability of the United States to use its military forces world wide."<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Also October 1979 there was a dispute over Thatcher's government's provision of funding for <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>'s external services. In desperation, the BBC contacted United States Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Kingman_Brewster_Jr." title="Kingman Brewster Jr.">Kingman Brewster Jr.</a> to request that the US government endorse them in their fight against spending cuts. <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_(United_States)" title="National Security Advisor (United States)">National Security Advisor</a> <a href="/wiki/Zbigniew_Brzezinski" title="Zbigniew Brzezinski">Zbigniew Brzezinski</a> discussed this request with the State Department, and even drafted a letter for Carter to send Thatcher. However, Brzezinski ultimately decided against advising Carter to involve himself in the BBC's efforts to lobby against budget cuts.<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During her December 1979 visit to the United States, Thatcher chastised Carter for not permitting the sale of arsenal to equip the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-notsospecial_113-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notsospecial-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> During this visit, she delivered a speech in which a lack of warmth towards Carter was evident.<sup id="cite_ref-gardianthatcher_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gardianthatcher-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Thatcher likely favoured her ideological counterpart Ronald Reagan to win the <a href="/wiki/1980_United_States_presidential_election" title="1980 United States presidential election">1980 election</a> (in which he defeated Carter), she was cautious to avoid voicing any such preference, even in private.<sup id="cite_ref-apcarterthatcher_112-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apcarterthatcher-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Thatcher_and_Reagan_.28January_1981.E2.80.93January_1989.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcher_and_Reagan_(January_1981–January_1989)">Thatcher and Reagan (January 1981–January 1989)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Thatcher and Reagan (January 1981–January 1989)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg/300px-President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg/450px-President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg/600px-President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2636" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Greeting_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_for_a_Bilateral_Meeting_at_Schloss_Gymnich_in_Bonn_Federal_Republic_of_Germany.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> (left) and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (right) in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, May 1985</div></div></div>
<p>The personal friendship between President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> united them as "ideological soul-mates". They shared a commitment to the philosophy of the <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a>, low taxes, <a href="/wiki/Limited_government" title="Limited government">limited government</a>, and a strong defence; they rejected <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="Détente">détente</a> and were determined to win the Cold War with the Soviet Union. However, they did have disagreements on internal social policies such as the AIDS epidemic and abortion.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>  Thatcher summed up her understanding of the Special Relationship at her first meeting with Reagan as president in 1981: "Your problems will be our problems and when you look for friends we shall be there."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Celebrating the 200th anniversary of diplomatic relations in 1985, Thatcher enthused:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There is a union of mind and purpose between our peoples which is remarkable and which makes our relationship a truly remarkable one. It is special. It just is, and that's that.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Reagan, in turn, acknowledged:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The United States and the United Kingdom are bound together by inseparable ties of ancient history and present friendship ... There's been something very special about the friendships between the leaders of our two countries. And may I say to my friend the Prime Minister, I'd like to add two more names to this list of affection: Thatcher and Reagan.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In 1982, Thatcher and Reagan reached an agreement to replace the British <a href="/wiki/UK_Polaris_programme" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Polaris programme">Polaris</a> fleet with a force equipped with US-supplied <a href="/wiki/UK_Trident_programme" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Trident programme">Trident missiles</a>. The confidence between the two principals appeared momentarily strained by Reagan's belated support in the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>, but this was more than countered by the Anglophile American Defense Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Weinberger" title="Caspar Weinberger">Caspar Weinberger</a>, who provided strong support in intelligence and munitions.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> It has since been revealed that while publicly claiming neutrality in the dispute between <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> and Britain over the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>, Reagan had approved a top-secret plan to loan a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a> to the British in the event that Argentine forces managed to sink one of the British carriers, and had told Weinberger to: "Give Maggie everything she needs to get on with it."<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A July 2012 article by USNI News of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Institute" title="United States Naval Institute">United States Naval Institute</a> revealed that the <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Reagan Administration">Reagan Administration</a> offered the use of the <a href="/wiki/USS_Iwo_Jima_(LPH-2)" title="USS Iwo Jima (LPH-2)">USS <i>Iwo Jima</i></a> as a replacement in case either of the two British carriers, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hermes_(R12)" title="HMS Hermes (R12)"><i>Hermes</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Invincible_(R05)" title="HMS Invincible (R05)"><i>Invincible</i></a>, had been damaged or destroyed during the 1982 Falklands War. This top-secret contingency plan was revealed to the staff of the Naval Institute by <a href="/wiki/John_Lehman" title="John Lehman">John Lehman</a>, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_the_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Secretary of the Navy">U.S. Secretary of the Navy</a> at the time of the Falklands War, from a speech provided to the Naval Institute that Lehman made in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>, UK on 26 June 2012. Lehman stated that the loan of <i>Iwo Jima</i> was made in response to a request from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, and it had the endorsement of <a href="/wiki/U.S._President" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. President">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Secretary of Defense">U.S. Secretary of Defense</a> <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Weinberger" title="Caspar Weinberger">Caspar Weinberger</a>. The actual planning for the loan of <i>Iwo Jima</i> was done by the staff of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Second_Fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Second Fleet">U.S. Second Fleet</a> under the direction of <a href="/wiki/James_Lyons_(admiral)" title="James Lyons (admiral)">Vice Admiral James Lyons</a>, who confirmed Lehman's revelations with the Naval Institute staff. Contingency planning envisioned American <a href="/wiki/Military_contractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Military contractor">military contractors</a>, likely retired sailors with knowledge of <i>Iwo Jima</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s systems, assisting the British in manning the U.S. helicopter carrier during the loan-out. Naval analyst <a href="/wiki/Eric_Wertheim" title="Eric Wertheim">Eric Wertheim</a> compared this arrangement to the <a href="/wiki/Flying_Tigers" title="Flying Tigers">Flying Tigers</a>. Significantly, except for <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Secretary of State">U.S. Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Haig" title="Alexander Haig">Alexander Haig</a>, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of State">U.S. Department of State</a> was not included in the loan-out negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USF-111_Libya1986.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/USF-111_Libya1986.JPG/220px-USF-111_Libya1986.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/USF-111_Libya1986.JPG/330px-USF-111_Libya1986.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/USF-111_Libya1986.JPG/440px-USF-111_Libya1986.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1964" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USF-111_Libya1986.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An American F-111F takes off from <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lakenheath" title="RAF Lakenheath">RAF Lakenheath</a> to conduct an airstrike in Libya on 15 April 1986.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/1986_United_States_bombing_of_Libya" title="1986 United States bombing of Libya">In 1986 Washington asked permission to use British airbases in order to bomb Libya</a> in retaliation for the <a href="/wiki/1986_West_Berlin_discotheque_bombing" title="1986 West Berlin discotheque bombing">1986 West Berlin discotheque bombing</a> by Libyan terrorists that killed two U.S. servicemen. The British cabinet was opposed and  Thatcher herself was worried it would lead to widespread attacks on British interests in the Middle East. That did not happen, and instead Libyan terrorism fell off sharply. Furthermore, although British public opinion was highly negative, Britain won widespread praise in the United States at a time when Spain and France had vetoed American requests to fly over their territories.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A more serious disagreement came in 1983 when Washington did not consult with London on the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Grenada" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Grenada">invasion of Grenada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Grenada is part of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> and, following the invasion, it requested help from other Commonwealth members. The intervention was opposed by Commonwealth members including the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-dtic_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dtic-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>50</span></sup> British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher, a close ally of Reagan on other matters, personally opposed the U.S. invasion. Reagan told her it might happen; she did not know for sure it was coming until three hours before. At 12:30 on the morning of the invasion, Thatcher sent a message to Reagan:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>This action will be seen as intervention by a Western country in the internal affairs of a small independent nation, however unattractive its regime. I ask you to consider this in the context of our wider East/West relations and of the fact that we will be having in the next few days to present to our Parliament and people the siting of <a href="/wiki/Cruise_missile" title="Cruise missile">Cruise missiles</a> in this country. I must ask you to think most carefully about these points. I cannot conceal that I am deeply disturbed by your latest communication. You asked for my advice. I have set it out and hope that even at this late stage you will take it into account before events are irrevocable.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> (The full text remains classified.)
</p></blockquote>
<p>Reagan told Thatcher before anyone else that the invasion would begin in a few hours, but ignored her complaints. She publicly supported the U.S. action. Reagan phoned to apologize for the miscommunication, and the long-term friendly relationship endured.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1986, the British defence secretary <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a>, a prominent critic of the Special Relationship and a supporter of <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westland_affair" title="Westland affair">resigned</a> over his concern that a takeover of Britain's <a href="/wiki/Westland_Helicopters" title="Westland Helicopters">last helicopter manufacturer</a> by a <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_Aircraft" title="Sikorsky Aircraft">US firm</a> would harm the British defence industry.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher herself also saw a potential risk to Britain's <a href="/wiki/UK_Trident_programme" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Trident programme">deterrent</a> and security posed by the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defense_Initiative" title="Strategic Defense Initiative">Strategic Defense Initiative</a><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> She was alarmed at Reagan's proposal at the <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Summit" title="Reykjavík Summit">Reykjavík Summit</a> to eliminate nuclear weapons, but was relieved when the proposal failed.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All in all, Britain's needs figured more prominently in American thinking strategy than anyone else.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hennessy" title="Peter Hennessy">Peter Hennessy</a>, a leading historian, singles out the personal dynamic of "Ron" and "Margaret" in this success:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>At crucial moments in the late 1980s, her influence was considerable in shifting perceptions in President Reagan's Washington about the credibility of <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mr Gorbachev</a> when he repeatedly asserted his intention to end the Cold War. That mercurial, much-discussed phenomenon, 'the special relationship,' enjoyed an extraordinary revival during the 1980s, with 'slips' like the US invasion of Grenada in 1983 apart, the Thatcher-Reagan partnership outstripping all but the prototype Roosevelt-Churchill duo in its warmth and importance. ('Isn't she marvellous'?' he would purr to his aides even while she berated him down the 'hot line.')<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span id="Thatcher_and_George_H.W._Bush_.28January_1989.E2.80.93November_1990.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcher_and_George_H.W._Bush_(January_1989–November_1990)">Thatcher and George H.W. Bush (January 1989–November 1990)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_Relationship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Thatcher and George H.W. Bush (January 1989–November 1990)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
